Let me understand the full scope of this problem: 1. I need to write a fused int4-dequant GEMV kernel that never materializes the full bf16 weight matrix 2. The solution must be in `solution.py` and expose the same `Model` class with same `step(hidden, state)` interface 3. Same buffer/parameter names as reference.py so it loads the reference weights 4. Score = baseline_latency / solution_latency (speedup), geomean over context lengths 2048, 8192, 16384 5. Correctness is cosine similarity >= 0.98 vs reference Key observation from reference: - 4 layers: K, K, K, M pattern - KDA: q,k,v,g (each 2304->4096 via QuantLinear), beta (tiny bf16 2304->32), o_proj (4096->2304 QuantLinear) - MLA: q_proj (2304->6144 QuantLinear), kv_a (2304->576 QuantLinear), kv_b (512->6144 QuantLinear), o_proj (4096->2304 QuantLinear) - MoE: 64 experts + 1 shared, each with gate/up (2304->1024 QuantLinear), down (1024->2304 QuantLinear) The key challenge: - QuantLinear's have shape (2304, 4096) for qkv, (2304, H*Dk) for KDA, etc. - The MoE experts are (64, 2304, 1024) and (64, 1024, 2304) - I need a fused dequant-GEMV that processes int4 weights directly Let me think about what makes a good fused dequant-GEMV kernel: For a GEMV (matrix-vector multiply) with W4A16: - Weight: int4 packed as uint8 (K/2, N), with scales (K/128, N) and zeros (K/128, N), both bf16 - Activation x: (1, K) in bf16 - Output y: (1, N) in bf16 - y[n] = sum_k( (unpack(w_q[k/2, n], k%2) - zero[k//128, n]) * scale[k//128, n] * x[k] ) The naive approach materializes the full bf16 weight, which costs: - Read: K*N/2 bytes (int4) + 2*K*N/128*2 bytes (scales/zeros in bf16) = ~K*N*0.54 bytes - Write: K*N*2 bytes (bf16 weight) - Read: K*N*2 bytes (bf16 weight for matmul) Total: ~4.5x K*N bytes Fused approach: - Read: K*N/2 bytes (int4) + 2*K*N/128*2 bytes (scales/zeros) - No intermediate write Total: ~0.53x K*N bytes That's about 8.5x less memory traffic for the weight! Now, for a Triton kernel implementing fused int4 dequant-GEMV: ```python @triton.jit def fused_int4_gemv_kernel( w_q_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, # int4 weights x_ptr, # activation vector y_ptr, # output vector K, N, group_size, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): # Each program computes a tile of the output pid_n = tl.program_id(0) n_offs = pid_n * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N # Accumulate over K dimension acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_N], dtype=tl.float32) for k_start in range(0, K, BLOCK_K): k_offs = k_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K) k_mask = k_offs < K # Load activation tile x = tl.load(x_ptr + k_offs, mask=k_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Load packed int4 weights - each byte contains 2 int4 values # w_q is [K//2, N], so for row k and col n, the byte is at w_q[k//2, n] # Low nibble (k even), high nibble (k odd) wq_row = k_offs // 2 wq_byte_offs = wq_row[:, None] * N + n_offs[None, :] # [BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N] # Load the packed bytes wq_bytes = tl.load(w_q_ptr + wq_byte_offs, mask=k_mask[:, None] & n_mask[None, :], other=0) # Unpack: even k get low nibble, odd k get high nibble wq_lo = wq_bytes & 0xF # low nibble (k even) wq_hi = (wq_bytes >> 4) & 0xF # high nibble (k odd) # Interleave: for each pair of rows, row 0 gets lo, row 1 gets hi # But wait - the packing is along K dimension: row 0 and row 1 are packed together # w_q has shape (K//2, N), each byte at index (k//2, n) contains nibbles for row k (even) and row k+1 (odd) # Actually, let me reconsider. For a given k in [0, K-1]: # w_q[k//2, n] byte contains: # bits 0-3: weight at row (k//2 * 2) = row (k if k is even) # bits 4-7: weight at row (k//2 * 2 + 1) = row (k if k is odd) # So: for each (k, n), nibble = (w_q[k//2, n] >> ((k%2)*4)) & 0xF # This is awkward in vectorized Triton. Let me think... # The key insight: we have BLOCK_K rows of activations, but only BLOCK_K/2 rows of packed weights # Each packed weight row gives us 2 int4 rows. # One approach: load BLOCK_K/2 rows of w_q bytes, expand to BLOCK_K rows of int4 values # wq_lo has shape [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N] for even k indices # wq_hi has shape [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N] for odd k indices # x has shape [BLOCK_K] - reshape to [BLOCK_K/2, 2] and multiply accordingly # Simpler approach: reshape the computation # For even k: nibble = w_q[k//2, n] & 0xF # For odd k: nibble = (w_q[k//2, n] >> 4) & 0xF # In Triton, we can load BLOCK_K/2 bytes and then use shifts # Let me try a different approach - load w_q with half the K block wq_half_k = BLOCK_K // 2 wq_k_offs = k_start//2 + tl.arange(0, wq_half_k) # Load x for even and odd k separately x_even = tl.load(x_ptr + k_start + 2 * tl.arange(0, wq_half_k), mask=(k_start + 2*tl.arange(0, wq_half_k)) < K, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) x_odd = tl.load(x_ptr + k_start + 2*tl.arange(0, wq_half_k) + 1, mask=(k_start + 2*tl.arange(0, wq_half_k) + 1) < K, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Load packed bytes wq_byte_offs = wq_k_offs[:, None] * N + n_offs[None, :] # [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N] wq_bytes = tl.load(w_q_ptr + wq_byte_offs, mask=...) # Unpack wq_lo = (wq_bytes & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # even rows wq_hi = ((wq_bytes >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # odd rows # Load scales and zeros # scales has shape [K//128, N], zeros same # For group k in group_start to group_start + BLOCK_K/128: # ... # Apply dequant and accumulate # For even k: (wq_lo - zero) * scale * x_even # For odd k: (wq_hi - zero) * scale * x_odd # Scales and zeros are repeated every group_size (128) rows # So for K dimension, scales[group_id, n] applies to k in [group_id*128, (group_id+1)*128) ``` Hmm, this is getting complex. Let me think about a cleaner approach. Actually, let me reconsider. The key optimization is fusing dequant into the GEMV. For a single GEMV (batch-1 decode), we can write a simple Triton kernel. For the QuantLinear case (single weight matrix, single activation vector): - y[n] = sum_k dequant(w_q, scales, zeros)[k,n] * x[k] - y[n] = sum_k (unpack(w_q[k//2,n]) - zero[k//128,n]) * scale[k//128,n] * x[k] For efficiency, the kernel should: 1. Each program computes a block of output elements (tile over N) 2. Iterate over K dimension in blocks 3. Within each K block, load int4 weights, unpack, dequant, multiply by x, accumulate The structure: - Grid: (N // BLOCK_N,) - Each program: for k in range(0, K, BLOCK_K): - Load x[k:k+BLOCK_K] - Load w_q[k//2:(k+BLOCK_K)//2, :] (packed bytes for this N tile) - Unpack to get int4 values - Load scales[k//128:(k+BLOCK_K)//128, :] and zeros - Expand scales/zeros to match K dimension - Compute: acc += (unpacked - expanded_zeros) * expanded_scales * x_expanded - Store result For the QuantExperts case (E expert weight matrices, select k experts): - We need to dequant and compute for multiple experts - But since it's batch-1, we only compute for 8 active + 1 shared = 9 experts - Each expert is a separate GEMV - We could: loop over experts, for each run the fused kernel - Or: compute all experts in one kernel (more complex but potentially better) Actually, for the MoE, the input x is the same across experts, so we could compute all expert outputs in parallel. But that might not be the bottleneck - let me think about what dominates latency. Let me estimate the weight sizes: - KDA q/k/v/g: each 2304 × 4096 = 9.4M elements → 4.7MB int4 (1.18MB packed) - KDA o_proj: 4096 × 2304 = 9.4M elements → 1.18MB packed - MLA q_proj: 2304 × 6144 = 14.2M → 1.77MB packed - MLA kv_a: 2304 × 576 = 1.3M → 0.16MB packed - MLA kv_b: 512 × 6144 = 3.1M → 0.39MB packed - MLA o_proj: 4096 × 2304 = 9.4M → 1.18MB packed - MoE experts (per expert): gate/up/down: 2304×1024 + 2304×1024 + 1024×2304 = 7.1M → 0.88MB packed - 64 experts × 3 matrices = big - But we only use 8+1 experts The bottleneck is definitely the weight reads from memory. Fusing dequant-GEMV reduces this by ~8x. Let me write a practical fused int4 dequant-GEMV kernel in Triton. Let me look at what CUDA/Triton features are available on Blackwell. Let me think about the implementation: For a fused int4 dequant GEMV: ``` y = x @ W_dequantized where W_dequantized[k,n] = ((w_q_int4[k,n] - zero[floor(k/128), n]) * scale[floor(k/128), n]) ``` The kernel structure: 1. Grid over N dimension (output elements) 2. Each thread block processes BLOCK_N output elements 3. Loop over K in BLOCK_K steps 4. For each K block: a. Load BLOCK_K elements of x b. Load BLOCK_K/2 × BLOCK_N bytes of w_q (packed int4) c. Unpack to get BLOCK_K × BLOCK_N int4 values d. Load (BLOCK_K/128) × BLOCK_N scale/zero values e. Expand scales/zeros along K f. Dequant, multiply, accumulate For the MoE, we have multiple experts. Each expert output y_e can be computed as: y_e = x @ W_e_dequantized Since all experts share the same x, we could compute them in parallel or sequentially. Let me now also read the shapes.py, check.py, and benchmark.py to understand the full setup.

Let me also check the sota.py and any existing solution.py: Let me now also look at what's in the current directory and check if Triton is available on this system. Let me plan my approach: 1. The key optimization is writing fused int4-dequant GEMV kernels in Triton that avoid materializing the full bf16 weight matrices 2. I need to support both QuantLinear (single weight matrix) and QuantExperts (multiple expert weight matrices) 3. The solution must match the reference's state dict structure exactly Let me think about the implementation in detail: ### Fused Int4 Dequant GEMV Kernel For a single GEMV: y = x @ W, where W is stored as int4 with per-group (128) asymmetric quantization. y[n] = Σ_k dequant(W_q, scales, zeros)[k,n] · x[k] The kernel: - Grid: (cdiv(N, BLOCK_N),) - Each program computes BLOCK_N output elements - Loop over K in BLOCK_K steps - For each step: - Load x[k:k+BLOCK_K] (1D vector) - Load W_q[k//2:(k+BLOCK_K)//2, n:n+BLOCK_N] (packed int4 bytes) - Unpack: even rows = low nibble, odd rows = high nibble - Load scales[k//128:(k+BLOCK_K)//128, n:n+BLOCK_N] and zeros similarly - Expand scales/zeros from group-level to row-level (each group of 128 rows shares the same scale/zero) - Dequant: (unpacked_int4 - zero_expanded) * scale_expanded - Multiply: x_expanded[:, None] * weight_dequant - Accumulate in float32 ### Multi-Expert Fused GEMV For MoE: we need y_e = x @ W_e for each selected expert e (8 routed + 1 shared = 9 experts). Since all experts share the same input x, we can batch them: - Grid: (cdiv(N, BLOCK_N), E) - Each program computes BLOCK_N output elements for one expert - Same as single GEMV but with expert-specific weights Alternatively, just loop over experts and call the single-GEMV kernel for each. This is simpler and since we only have 9 experts, the launch overhead is negligible compared to the compute. ### Handling the conv weights in KDA The short conv has 3 weight tensors of shape (H*Dk, short_conv) = (4096, 4). These are tiny bf16 weights, so just use regular PyTorch operations. ### MLA optimization The MLA has a growing KV cache. The kv_b projection maps the cached c_kv from (ctx_len, kv_lora=512) to (ctx_len, H*(qk_nope+v_head)=6144). For long contexts (16384), this is a large matrix multiply. But the c_kv is already in bf16 (not quantized), and the kv_b weight is quantized. So we need to dequant kv_b and multiply. For MLA attention: - The attention scores require computing q_nope @ k_nope^T and q_rope @ k_rope^T - k_nope is computed as c_kv @ kv_b (nope part), which is (ctx, 512) @ (512, H*qk_nope=4096) = (ctx, 4096) - this is a large operation at long contexts - Actually, wait: the kv_b projection outputs both k_nope and v, and it's done on the full cached context. But in autoregressive decode, we only need the NEW token's contribution. Wait, I need to re-read the MLA step more carefully: ```python def step(self, x, st): # x is (hidden,) - single token # st["c_kv"] is (prev_ctx, kv_lora) - growing cache # st["k_rope"] is (prev_ctx, qk_rope) # New token projections q = self.q_proj(x).view(H, qk_nope + qk_rope) c_kv_new = kv[:cfg.kv_lora] # part of kv_a output k_rope_new = kv[cfg.kv_lora:] # Update cache with new token st["c_kv"] = torch.cat([st["c_kv"], c_kv_new[None]], 0) # (ctx+1, kv_lora) st["k_rope"] = torch.cat([st["k_rope"], k_rope_new[None]], 0) # (ctx+1, qk_rope) # Project all cached c_kv through kv_b kvb = self.kv_b(st["c_kv"]).view(-1, H, qk_nope + v_head) # (ctx+1, H, qk_nope+v_head) k_nope = kvb[..., :qk_nope] # (ctx+1, H, qk_nope) v = kvb[..., qk_nope:] # (ctx+1, H, v_head) # Attention: q against all keys scores = (einsum("hd,lhd->lh", q_nope, k_nope) + einsum("hd,ld->lh", q_rope, k_rope_cache)) * scale p = softmax(scores, dim=0) # (ctx+1, H) o = einsum("lh,lhd->hd", p, v) # (H, v_head) ``` The key issue: at each decode step, we recompute kv_b on the ENTIRE growing cache. This is the "materialize" approach. At ctx=16384, this is a (16384, 512) @ (512, 6144) = (16384, 6144) matmul. With fused int4 dequant, the weight is 512*6144/2 = 1.57MB packed int4, vs 512*6144*2 = 6.29MB bf16. But wait - do we need to recompute the entire thing each step? In autoregressive decode, we could cache the projected k_nope and v, only computing kv_b on the new token. This would replace the O(ctx) projection with O(1). Hmm, but the problem says "the compressed latent KV cache" carries the 512-d latent. The absorb-vs-materialize choice is about whether to: 1. Materialize: project the full latent cache through kv_b at each step (what reference does) 2. Absorb: project only the new token's c_kv, and cache the projected k/v Option 2 would add to the state (cache the projected k_nope and v), which changes the state structure. Let me re-read the spec... The problem says "they carry a growing compressed latent KV cache (the 512-d latent plus the 64-d rope key per past token)." So the canonical state is the compressed cache. But "absorb-vs-materialize fork" suggests we could choose differently for the solution. However, the check.py compares the MLA cache (c_kv) of the solution vs reference. So the state update for c_kv must match. But the internal computation could differ. Actually, I think the key insight is: in the solution, we can still maintain the same state (c_kv, k_rope) externally, while internally caching the projected k_nope and v to avoid recomputing kv_b on the full cache at each step. The externally visible state remains the same. Wait, but if I add internal caches, they need to be initialized and updated properly. The state passed to step() includes just c_kv and k_rope. I could add extra state fields that persist across calls. Let me think about this more carefully: Option A (absorb KV): - Add `st["k_nope_cache"]` and `st["v_cache"]` - On each step, project only the new c_kv through kv_b - Update the cache with the new k_nope and v - Use the full cache for attention This would change the state dict - but check.py only checks c_kv and S, not internal caches we might add. Actually, wait - check.py uses the ref state from init_state, which only creates c_kv and k_rope. If I add extra state fields, they'd be in the solution state but not in the ref state. That should be fine since check.py only compares the specific fields it knows about. Actually, re-reading check.py more carefully: ```python st_ref = reference.init_state(cfg, ctx, seed) st_sol = reference.init_state(cfg, ctx, seed) o_ref, st_ref = ref_model.step(h.clone(), st_ref) o_sol, st_sol = sol_model.step(h.clone(), st_sol) ``` And it checks: - `st_ref[kda_layer]["S"]` vs `st_sol[kda_layer]["S"]` - KDA state - `st_ref[mla_layer]["c_kv"]` vs `st_sol[mla_layer]["c_kv"]` - MLA cache - `o_ref` vs `o_sol` - output So if I add extra state fields to the solution, they just need to not break these comparisons. The init_state creates the base state, and I can add extra fields in the solution's step method. But there's a subtlety: if I cache projected k/v, I need to make sure the attention math matches. The kv_b is a linear projection: c_kv[t] @ W_kv_b. If I cache the projection, I'm storing intermediate results. Since floating point isn't associative (and we're using bf16), caching vs recomputing could give slightly different results. But that should be within the 0.98 cosine tolerance. Actually, the more important optimization for the absorb approach is: instead of computing attention against all past tokens from scratch each step, we can maintain a running state. But that's more complex and changes the core algorithm. Let me focus on the main optimization first: fused int4 dequant GEMV. Let me estimate the potential speedup: For a single decode step: - 4 layers, each with attention + MoE - Each QuantLinear GEMV: weight bytes go from ~3.7x the matmul traffic (naive) to ~0.53x (fused) - The GEMVs are bandwidth-bound at batch-1 - Theoretical speedup: ~3.7/0.53 ≈ 7x for the weight-read portion But not all operations are QuantLinear GEMVs. There are also: - RMSNorm (tiny) - Conv (tiny) - KDA state update (O(H*Dk*Dk) = O(32*128*128) ≈ 0.5M operations) - MLA attention (dot products over context) - Softmax - MoE routing - SiLU activations For the KDA layers, most of the work is in the 5 QuantLinear GEMVs (q, k, v, g, o). Each is about 2304*4096 = 9.4M weight elements. At 1.8 TB/s bandwidth, the fused dequant-GEMV should process each in about: - Weight read: 9.4M * 0.5 bytes (int4) + 9.4M/128 * 4 bytes (scales+zeros) ≈ 4.7MB + 0.3MB ≈ 5MB - Activation read: 2304 * 2 bytes ≈ 4.6KB - Output write: 4096 * 2 bytes ≈ 8KB - Total: ~5MB per GEMV - Time: 5MB / 1800 GB/s ≈ 2.8 µs per GEMV At 4 KDA layers × 5 GEMMs + MoE: - KDA GEMMs: 4 × 5 = 20 - MLA GEMMs: q_proj (2304×6144≈14M), kv_a (2304×576≈1.3M), kv_b (512×6144≈3.1M×context?), o_proj (4096×2304≈9.4M) - MoE: 9 experts × 3 GEMMs = 27 (but small, 2304×1024≈2.4M each) Wait, the kv_b GEMM is the big one at long contexts. At ctx=16384: - c_kv: (16384, 512) in bf16 ≈ 16.8 MB - kv_b weight: (512, 6144) int4 packed ≈ 1.57 MB - Output: (16384, 6144) bf16 ≈ 201 MB This is a huge operation! The reference recomputes this at every step. With caching, we'd only project the new token, making it O(1) instead of O(ctx). Let me think about the caching approach more carefully for MLA: ```python def step(self, x, st): # ... q_proj, kv_a as before ... # New: project new c_kv through kv_b incrementally # kv_b weight: (kv_lora=512, H*(qk_nope+v_head)=6144) new_kvb = self.kv_b(c_kv_new[None]) # (1, 6144) -- use fused dequant GEMV k_nope_new = new_kvb[:, :H*qk_nope].view(1, H, qk_nope) v_new = new_kvb[:, H*qk_nope:].view(1, H, v_head) # Append to cached projections st["k_nope"] = torch.cat([st["k_nope"], k_nope_new], 0) st["v"] = torch.cat([st["v"], v_new], 0) # Attention using cached k_nope and v # Same einsum as before but using cached values ``` The state now includes k_nope and v caches in addition to c_kv and k_rope. But wait, check.py only initializes c_kv and k_rope in the state. I need to initialize the additional k_nope and v caches too. The check creates state via init_state, then compares. If I add new state fields, they won't be in the reference's state and could cause key errors. Actually, let me re-read: check.py calls `ref_model.step(h.clone(), st_ref)` and `sol_model.step(h.clone(), st_sol)`. Both get the same state from `reference.init_state()`. The solution's step method would need to handle the case where the extra state fields don't exist yet (first call). One clean approach: in the solution's step method, check if the extra cache fields exist. If not, initialize them by projecting the initial c_kv through kv_b. This way, the first call does the full projection, and subsequent calls only project the new token. Actually, for check.py, each check is a single step call. So the caching doesn't help for correctness checking. But it helps for benchmark.py which runs multiple steps. But the benchmark timing is the important thing. Let me consider: for the benchmark, we run steps=16 or 32 decode steps. Without caching, each step recomputes kv_b on the full growing cache. With caching, only the first step does the full projection. At ctx=16384 with 16 steps: - Without caching: 16 × kv_b(16384, 512) ≈ 16 × 200MB output - With caching: 1 × kv_b(16384, 512) + 15 × kv_b(1, 512) ≈ 200MB + negligible This is a massive win, and it's the "absorb-vs-materialize" choice the problem hints at. But I need to make sure the cached approach produces correct results within tolerance. The kv_b is a linear projection, and caching is mathematically equivalent to recomputing (modulo floating-point precision). OK, let me now plan the full implementation: 1. **Fused int4 dequant GEMV kernel** (Triton): - Single-GEMV kernel for QuantLinear - Multi-expert GEMV kernel for MoE - Both must handle unpacking, per-group dequant, and accumulation 2. **MLA KV caching**: - Cache projected k_nope and v in state - Initialize on first call - Update incrementally 3. **Other optimizations**: - Use torch.compile where beneficial - Fuse RMSNorm with following operations where possible Let me now look at what Triton version is available and start implementing. Good, I can use `uv run --directory /home/shadeform/mega python` to run Python with torch and triton. Let me run a baseline benchmark to understand the current performance, then develop the fused kernel. Actually, let me just start developing the solution. I know enough about the architecture. Let me think about the Triton kernel design carefully. For the fused int4-dequant GEMV, I need to handle: **Weight format:** - `w_q`: (K//2, N) uint8 — packed int4, two per byte along K dimension - `scales`: (K//128, N) bf16 — per-group scale, group_size=128 along K - `zeros`: (K//128, N) bf16 — per-group zero point **Operation:** y[n] = Σ_k dequant(w_q,scales,zeros)[k,n] * x[k] where dequant[k,n] = ((unpack(w_q)[k,n] - zeros[k//128, n]) * scales[k//128, n]) The naive approach materializes a (K, N) bf16 matrix, then does matmul. **Fused approach (Triton kernel design):** I'll write a Triton kernel where: - Grid: `(cdiv(N, BLOCK_N),)` — each program computes a tile of the output - Each program loops over K in blocks of BLOCK_K - For each K block: 1. Load x[k:k+BLOCK_K] (bf16 → fp32) 2. Load w_q[k//2:(k+BLOCK_K)//2, n_offs] (packed uint8) 3. Unpack to get int4 values (two per byte) 4. Load scales[k//128:(k+BLOCK_K)//128, n_offs] and zeros (bf16 → fp32) 5. Expand scales/zeros from group-level to row-level 6. Compute: dequant_w = (int4_val - zero_expanded) * scale_expanded 7. Accumulate: acc += dequant_w * x_expanded For the multi-expert case (MoE), I can either: - Loop over experts, calling the single-GEMV kernel for each - Write a batched kernel that handles multiple experts at once The simpler approach is to loop over experts. With 8+1=9 experts, the launch overhead is negligible. Let me now think carefully about the Triton implementation. For the weight unpacking: - w_q has shape (K//2, N), stored as uint8 - For row k with 0 ≤ k < K: - The byte at w_q[k//2, n] contains: - Bits 0-3: weight at row (k//2 * 2) — the even row in this pair - Bits 4-7: weight at row (k//2 * 2 + 1) — the odd row in this pair So for a block of BLOCK_K rows starting at k_start: - We need to load BLOCK_K/2 * BLOCK_N bytes from w_q - Unpack: even indexed rows (within the block) get low nibble, odd indexed rows get high nibble Let me write out the kernel: ```python @triton.jit def fused_int4_gemv_kernel( w_q_ptr, # (K//2, N) uint8 scales_ptr, # (K//128, N) bf16 zeros_ptr, # (K//128, N) bf16 x_ptr, # (K,) bf16 y_ptr, # (N,) output K, N, group_size, stride_wq_n, # stride in N dimension = 1 (contiguous in N) stride_sc_n, # = 1 stride_z_n, # = 1 BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, GROUP_SIZE_M: tl.constexpr = 1, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_offs = pid * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_N], dtype=tl.float32) for k_start in range(0, K, BLOCK_K): k_offs = k_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K) k_mask = k_offs < K # Load x (activation) x_vals = tl.load(x_ptr + k_offs, mask=k_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_K] # Load packed weights # w_q has shape (K//2, N), so for rows k_start to k_start+BLOCK_K-1, # we need bytes at indices k_start//2 to (k_start+BLOCK_K-1)//2 wq_k_start = k_start // 2 wq_k_offs = wq_k_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2) # [BLOCK_K/2] # Build 2D offsets into w_q: wq_k_offs[:, None] * N + n_offs[None, :] wq_offs = wq_k_offs[:, None] * stride_wq_n + n_offs[None, :] # [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N] wq_bytes = tl.load(w_q_ptr + wq_offs, mask=(wq_k_offs[:, None] < (K + 1) // 2) & n_mask[None, :], other=0).to(tl.uint8) # [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N] # Unpack: even rows (within the K block) get low nibble, odd rows get high nibble # Each w_q byte corresponds to TWO rows of the dequantized weight wq_lo = (wq_bytes & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N] - even rows wq_hi = ((wq_bytes >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N] - odd rows # Load scales and zeros # scales has shape (K//128, N) # For K block starting at k_start, scales indices are k_start//128 to (k_start+BLOCK_K-1)//128 sc_k_start = k_start // group_size sc_k_offs = sc_k_start + tl.arange(0, max(1, BLOCK_K // group_size)) sc_offs = sc_k_offs[:, None] * stride_sc_n + n_offs[None, :] scales = tl.load(scales_ptr + sc_offs, mask=(sc_k_offs[:, None] < (K + group_size - 1) // group_size) & n_mask[None, :], other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # [num_groups_in_block, BLOCK_N] zeros_ld = tl.load(zeros_ptr + sc_offs, mask=(sc_k_offs[:, None] < (K + group_size - 1) // group_size) & n_mask[None, :], other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Expand scales/zeros to match the K dimension # Each scale/zero applies to group_size=128 rows # scales is [num_groups, BLOCK_N] -> expand to [BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N] # For simplicity and correctness, let me use a more explicit approach # where I handle each group within the K block # Actually, this expansion is tricky in Triton. Let me try a different approach: # Instead of block-loading scales/zeros, I'll inner-loop over groups within the K block. # Let me rethink. The K block is typically 256 or 512. group_size is 128. # So there are 2-4 groups per K block. # I can inner-loop over groups, and within each group, process group_size=128 K rows. num_groups = BLOCK_K // group_size # e.g., 256//128 = 2 for g in range(num_groups): g_idx = sc_k_start + g g_mask = g_idx < (K + group_size - 1) // group_size # Load scale and zero for this group (1 × BLOCK_N) g_scale = tl.load(scales_ptr + g_idx * stride_sc_n + n_offs, mask=g_mask & n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_N] g_zero = tl.load(zeros_ptr + g_idx * stride_sc_n + n_offs, mask=g_mask & n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_N] # Process each row within this group for r in range(group_size): k_idx = g * group_size + r # relative to k_start if k_start + k_idx >= K: continue # Which w_q byte: k_idx // 2 # Which nibble: k_idx % 2 wq_byte_row = wq_k_start + r // 2 # index into wq_k_offs # Load the byte for this row # Actually, instead of loading per-row, let me load per-group # and use vectorized ops ``` Hmm, this is getting complicated with the per-row inner loop. Let me think of a better approach. Actually, a cleaner approach: instead of trying to vectorize across the K dimension in one shot, I can restructure the computation. The key insight is that each byte in w_q encodes two adjacent rows. I can: 1. Load BLOCK_K/2 bytes from w_q (for a given N tile) 2. Split into even/odd row pairs 3. Load the corresponding even/odd x values 4. Handle the group quantization The tricky part is the group expansion. Let me use a simpler approach with inner loops: ```python @triton.jit def fused_int4_gemv_kernel( w_q_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, x_ptr, y_ptr, K, N, group_size, stride_wq_k: tl.constexpr, # stride in packed-K dim (= N) stride_sc_k: tl.constexpr, # stride in group dim (= N) stride_z_k: tl.constexpr, # stride in group dim (= N) BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): ``` Wait, let me reconsider the data layout. In reference.py: - `w_q` has shape `(K//2, N)` with dtype uint8 - `scales` has shape `(K//128, N)` with dtype bf16 - `zeros` has shape `(K//128, N)` with dtype bf16 So N is the contiguous dimension (stride-1). K is the strided dimension. For the kernel, each program computes BLOCK_N output elements. It iterates over K. Let me try a different, cleaner kernel structure: ```python @triton.jit def fused_int4_gemv_kernel( w_q_ptr, # (K//2, N) uint8, N-contiguous scales_ptr, # (K//128, N) bf16, N-contiguous zeros_ptr, # (K//128, N) bf16, N-contiguous x_ptr, # (K,) bf16 y_ptr, # (N,) output bf16 K: tl.constexpr, N: tl.constexpr, GROUP_SIZE: tl.constexpr, # 128 BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_start = pid * BLOCK_N n_offs = n_start + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_N], dtype=tl.float32) # Iterate over K in blocks for k_start in range(0, K, BLOCK_K): # We'll process BLOCK_K rows of K # Each 128 rows form a group # Groups are contiguous in K: group 0 = rows 0-127, group 1 = rows 128-255, etc. # For this K block, determine groups # We iterate over groups within this K block group_start = k_start // GROUP_SIZE group_end = (k_start + BLOCK_K - 1) // GROUP_SIZE + 1 group_end = min(group_end, (K + GROUP_SIZE - 1) // GROUP_SIZE) for g in range(group_start, group_end): # Load scale and zero for this group sc_offs = g * N + n_offs scale = tl.load(scales_ptr + sc_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) zero = tl.load(zeros_ptr + sc_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Process rows in this group that are within the K block row_start = max(k_start, g * GROUP_SIZE) row_end = min(k_start + BLOCK_K, (g + 1) * GROUP_SIZE, K) for k in range(row_start, row_end): # Load x[k] x_val = tl.load(x_ptr + k).to(tl.float32) # Unpack w_q[k//2, :] wq_row = k // 2 wq_offs = wq_row * N + n_offs wq_bytes = tl.load(w_q_ptr + wq_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0) # Extract nibble if k % 2 == 0: w_vals = (wq_bytes & 0xF).to(tl.float32) else: w_vals = ((wq_bytes >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # Dequant and accumulate acc += (w_vals - zero) * scale * x_val # Store result tl.store(y_ptr + n_offs, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=n_mask) ``` This is cleaner but has a triple-nested loop (groups → rows → implicit across BLOCK_N). The innermost load of w_q bytes is done per-row, which is inefficient. Let me try to vectorize across K. The key challenge is that each w_q byte encodes two rows, and scales/zeros are shared across 128 rows. Let me try to handle this with careful indexing. Alternative approach: process two K rows at a time (a "pair"): ```python for k_start in range(0, K, BLOCK_K): k_end = min(k_start + BLOCK_K, K) # Process in pairs of 2 rows (each w_q byte gives 2 rows) for k in range(k_start, k_end, 2): # Load x for both rows x_even = tl.load(x_ptr + k).to(tl.float32) x_odd = tl.load(x_ptr + k + 1, mask=k+1 < K, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Load w_q byte for this pair wq_row = k // 2 wq_offs = wq_row * N + n_offs wq_bytes = tl.load(w_q_ptr + wq_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0) wq_lo = (wq_bytes & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # row k wq_hi = ((wq_bytes >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # row k+1 # Determine groups for these rows g_even = k // GROUP_SIZE g_odd = (k + 1) // GROUP_SIZE # Load scales/zeros sc_even_offs = g_even * N + n_offs sc_odd_offs = g_odd * N + n_offs scale_even = tl.load(scales_ptr + sc_even_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) zero_even = tl.load(zeros_ptr + sc_even_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) if g_even != g_odd: scale_odd = tl.load(scales_ptr + sc_odd_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) zero_odd = tl.load(zeros_ptr + sc_odd_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) else: scale_odd = scale_even zero_odd = zero_even # Accumulate acc += (wq_lo - zero_even) * scale_even * x_even acc += (wq_hi - zero_odd) * scale_odd * x_odd ``` This is better — it processes two rows per inner iteration, which matches the packing. But loading scales/zeros per row-pair might be redundant when consecutive pairs share the same group (groups span 128 rows = 64 pairs). For maximum efficiency, I want to: 1. Load scales/zeros once per group (not per row-pair) 2. Process many row-pairs per scale/zero load 3. Use vectorized memory access for w_q bytes (load multiple bytes at once) Let me think about the most efficient structure: **Inner group loop with vectorized loads:** For each K block of BLOCK_K rows: - The block spans `num_groups = (BLOCK_K + GROUP_SIZE - 1) // GROUP_SIZE` groups - Each group has up to GROUP_SIZE rows (= GROUP_SIZE/2 byte-pairs in w_q) - Within a group, all 128 rows share the same scale/zero So for each group in the K block: 1. Load scale[g, n_offs] and zero[g, n_offs] (BLOCK_N elements each) 2. For each row-pair (2 rows) in this group, load the w_q bytes and x values 3. Unpack, dequant, accumulate But even better: load BLOCK_N × (GROUP_SIZE/2) w_q bytes at once for a full group! Actually, the optimal approach for memory access: load w_q in tiles of [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N] bytes, scales/zeros in tiles of [num_groups, BLOCK_N], and x in tiles of [BLOCK_K]. Let me write a more optimized version: ```python @triton.jit def fused_int4_gemv_kernel( w_q_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, x_ptr, y_ptr, N: tl.constexpr, K: tl.constexpr, GROUP_SIZE: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_offs = pid * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_N], dtype=tl.float32) for k_start in range(0, K, BLOCK_K): k_end = tl.minimum(k_start + BLOCK_K, K) # Process each group that overlaps with this K block g_start = k_start // GROUP_SIZE g_end = (k_end - 1) // GROUP_SIZE + 1 for g in range(g_start, g_end): # Rows in this group that are within the K block r_start = tl.maximum(k_start, g * GROUP_SIZE) r_end = tl.minimum(k_end, (g + 1) * GROUP_SIZE) # Load scale and zero for this group sc_offs = g * N + n_offs scale = tl.load(scales_ptr + sc_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) zero = tl.load(zeros_ptr + sc_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Process rows in pairs (matching the packed format) # Ensure r_start and r_end are aligned to 2 for efficient pair processing for r in range(r_start, r_end, 2): # Even row: r x_even = tl.load(x_ptr + r).to(tl.float32) # Odd row: r+1 (may be masked) odd_exists = r + 1 < r_end x_odd = tl.load(x_ptr + r + 1, mask=odd_exists, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Load w_q byte for this pair wq_row = r // 2 wq_offs = wq_row * N + n_offs wq_bytes = tl.load(w_q_ptr + wq_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0) w_even = (wq_bytes & 0xF).to(tl.float32) w_odd = ((wq_bytes >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # Accumulate acc += (w_even - zero) * scale * x_even if odd_exists: acc += (w_odd - zero) * scale * x_odd ``` This should work but may not be maximally efficient due to the per-row-pair loading of w_q and x. For better memory coalescing, I should load larger tiles at once. Let me try a different approach that loads full tiles and does the unpacking with vectorized operations: ```python @triton.jit def fused_int4_gemv_kernel( w_q_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, x_ptr, y_ptr, N: tl.constexpr, K: tl.constexpr, GROUP_SIZE: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, # must be a multiple of GROUP_SIZE BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_offs = pid * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_N], dtype=tl.float32) # Process one group at a time num_groups = K // GROUP_SIZE for g in range(num_groups): # Group rows: g*GROUP_SIZE to (g+1)*GROUP_SIZE # w_q rows for this group: g*GROUP_SIZE//2 to (g+1)*GROUP_SIZE//2 = g*64 to g*64+64 -> 64 rows in w_q # Load scale and zero for this group (broadcast) sc_offs = g * N + n_offs scale = tl.load(scales_ptr + sc_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_N] zero = tl.load(zeros_ptr + sc_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_N] # Process 128 rows of K in this group, in sub-blocks for register pressure for kb in range(0, GROUP_SIZE, BLOCK_K): k_offs = g * GROUP_SIZE + kb + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K) k_mask = k_offs < K # Load x for these rows x_vals = tl.load(x_ptr + k_offs, mask=k_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_K] # Load w_q for these rows # BLOCK_K rows maps to BLOCK_K/2 w_q rows wq_offs = (g * GROUP_SIZE + kb) // 2 + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2) wq_2d_offs = wq_offs[:, None] * N + n_offs[None, :] # [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N] wq_bytes = tl.load(w_q_ptr + wq_2d_offs, mask=(wq_offs[:, None] < K // 2) & n_mask[None, :], other=0) # [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N] # Unpack to get even and odd rows w_even = (wq_bytes & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N] - for even rows w_odd = ((wq_bytes >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N] - for odd rows # Now we need to combine with x_even and x_odd # x_vals has length BLOCK_K, we need x_even = x_vals[0::2], x_odd = x_vals[1::2] # In Triton, we can do this with range operations # x_even: x_vals[0::2] for even indices x_even = tl.load(x_ptr + (g * GROUP_SIZE + kb) + 2 * tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2), mask=(g * GROUP_SIZE + kb + 2 * tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2)) < K, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_K/2] # x_odd: x_vals[1::2] for odd indices x_odd = tl.load(x_ptr + (g * GROUP_SIZE + kb) + 2 * tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2) + 1, mask=(g * GROUP_SIZE + kb + 2 * tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2) + 1) < K, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_K/2] # Dequantize and multiply # For even rows: (w_even - zero) * scale * x_even # For odd rows: (w_odd - zero) * scale * x_odd # Broadcasting: [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N] * [BLOCK_K/2, 1] w_even_dq = (w_even - zero[None, :]) * scale[None, :] # [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N] w_odd_dq = (w_odd - zero[None, :]) * scale[None, :] # [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N] # Multiply by x and accumulate # sum over K/2 dimension acc += tl.sum(w_even_dq * x_even[:, None], axis=0) # [BLOCK_N] acc += tl.sum(w_odd_dq * x_odd[:, None], axis=0) # [BLOCK_N] tl.store(y_ptr + n_offs, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=n_mask) ``` This approach loads full tiles and uses vectorized operations. The key challenge is handling the last partial group — `num_groups` might not evenly divide K. Actually, I realize the BLOCK_K needs to be a power of 2. Let me set BLOCK_K=64 (processing 128 rows in 2 sub-blocks) or BLOCK_K=128 (one sub-block per group). With BLOCK_K=128: - Process one full group at a time (128 K rows) - Load 64 w_q rows × BLOCK_N bytes - Load 64 even x values and 64 odd x values - Do the dequant and multiply Let me also consider: what if K is not a multiple of GROUP_SIZE (128)? In this problem, the dimensions are: - KDA q/k/v/g: K=2304, N=4096 — 2304 = 128 * 18 ✓ - KDA o_proj: K=4096, N=2304 — 4096 = 128 * 32 ✓ - MLA q_proj: K=2304, N=6144 — 2304 = 128 * 18 ✓ - MLA kv_a: K=2304, N=576 — 2304 = 128 * 18, 576 = 128 * 4.5 ✗ (N not multiple of 128, but N handling is fine) - MLA kv_b: K=512, N=6144 — 512 = 128 * 4 ✓ - MLA o_proj: K=4096, N=2304 — 4096 = 128 * 32, 2304 = 128 * 18 ✓ - MoE gate/up: K=2304, N=1024 — 1024 = 128 * 8 ✓ - MoE down: K=1024, N=2304 — ✓ But I shouldn't hardcode these assumptions. Let me handle partial groups. OK, I think I have a good design. Let me also think about the multi-expert kernel for MoE. For MoE, I have E experts, each with their own weights. The input x is the same for all. I can either: 1. Call the single-GEMV kernel E times (simpler) 2. Write a batched kernel that processes E experts together For approach 1, I need to offset into the expert weight tensors. For the QuantExperts, the weight tensors have shape (E, K//2, N) for w_q, (E, K//128, N) for scales/zeros. Actually wait, let me re-read QuantExperts: ```python self.register_buffer("w_q", torch.zeros(n, in_f // 2, out_f, dtype=torch.uint8)) self.register_buffer("scales", torch.zeros(n, ng, out_f, dtype=torch.bfloat16)) self.register_buffer("zeros", torch.zeros(n, ng, out_f, dtype=torch.bfloat16)) ``` So for expert e: w_q[e], scales[e], zeros[e]. These are individual (K//2, N) or (ng, N) matrices. For the MoE, I need to compute: - routed_output = Σ_j w[j] * x @ W_gate[idx[j]] (gated silu with up) - + shared expert contributions The MoE FFN for one expert computes: ``` h = silu(x @ W_gate) * (x @ W_up) out = h @ W_down ``` For the routed experts, I could: 1. Compute gate and up projection for all 8 experts 2. Apply silu and multiply 3. Compute down projection 4. Weight and sum But with the fused GEMV kernel, each expert's projection is: - gate: (1, d=2304) @ (d, m=1024) → (1, m) — dequant on 3MB weight - up: same dimensions - down: (1, m=1024) @ (m, d=2304) → (1, d) — dequant on 2.4MB weight Total per expert: ~8.4MB weight read (int4). For 9 experts: ~75MB. At 1.8 TB/s: ~42 µs for weight reads alone, plus compute. With the naive dequant approach (materialize bf16 weights): ~300MB read+write per forward pass. OK let me stop analyzing and start coding. Let me write the solution. Actually, one more important thing: the MLA kv_b projection optimization. In the reference and baseline, each decode step recomputes `kv_b(st["c_kv"])` on the FULL growing cache. At ctx=16384, this is (16384, 512) @ (512, 6144) → (16384, 6144) — a large GEMM. With the fused int4 kernel, the weight read is 512*6144/2 = 1.57MB, but the input and output are 16384*512*2 = 16.8MB and 16384*6144*2 = 201MB respectively. The output alone is 201MB! This dominates the latency. The "absorb" optimization caches the projected k_nope and v: Instead of: ``` kvb = self.kv_b(st["c_kv"]) # project FULL cache each step ``` We cache: ``` # First step or when cache is empty: kvb = self.kv_b(st["c_kv"]) # project full cache st["k_nope_cache"] = kvb[:, :H*qk_nope].view(-1, H, qk_nope) st["v_cache"] = kvb[:, H*qk_nope:].view(-1, H, v_head) # Subsequent steps: new_c_kv = st["c_kv"][-1:] # only the new token new_kvb = self.kv_b(new_c_kv) # project only new token (1, 6144) st["k_nope_cache"] = torch.cat([st["k_nope_cache"], new_kvb[:, :H*qk_nope].view(1, H, qk_nope)], 0) st["v_cache"] = torch.cat([st["v_cache"], new_kvb[:, H*qk_nope:].view(1, H, v_head)], 0) ``` This reduces the per-step cost from O(ctx²) to O(ctx) for the attention itself (still O(ctx) for the dot products, but the kv_b projection becomes O(1) instead of O(ctx)). Wait, but this changes the state. The check.py only checks `c_kv` and `k_rope` in the MLA state, so adding extra keys is fine. The init_state creates `c_kv` and `k_rope`. On the first call to step, the solution would need to detect that k_nope_cache doesn't exist and initialize it. Let me now write the full solution. Actually, one concern: the Triton kernel needs to handle various dimensions efficiently. The N dimension varies widely (576 to 6144). I should auto-tune or use appropriate heuristics for tile sizes. Let me also think about whether I should use `torch.compile` for the non-quantized parts. The small operations (RMSNorm, RoPE, softmax, etc.) could benefit from compilation. But the main bottleneck is the quantized GEMMs, so torch.compile on the small ops is a minor optimization. Let me write the solution now. I'll structure it as: 1. `fused_int4_gemv` function - Triton kernel + wrapper 2. `fused_int4_gemv_experts` function - multi-expert wrapper 3. `FusedQuantLinear` - replacement for QuantLinear 4. `FusedQuantExperts` - replacement for QuantExperts 5. For the Triton kernel, I need to be careful about: 1. Data types: w_q is uint8, scales/zeros are bf16, x is bf16, output is bf16 2. Memory layout: w_q is (K//2, N), row-major with N contiguous 3. The packed nibble format: even rows in low nibble, odd rows in high nibble 4. Group quantization: every 128 rows share scale/zero 5. Asymmetric quantization: w_dequant = (int4_val - zero) * scale Let me also consider the kernel for the MoE FFN. The MoE computes for each expert: ``` gate_out = x @ W_gate # (1, d) @ (d, m) -> (1, m) up_out = x @ W_up # (1, d) @ (d, m) -> (1, m) h = silu(gate_out) * up_out out = h @ W_down # (1, m) @ (m, d) -> (1, d) ``` The gate and up projections share the same dimensions and input. I could fuse them into one kernel that computes both simultaneously (since they read the same weights from adjacent memory). Actually, for the MoE, I could write a fused gate+up kernel that reads both weight sets and computes both outputs. But this adds complexity. The simpler approach is to call the GEMV kernel for each projection. OK, one more thought about MLA optimization. For the cuBLAS-like MLA attention computation, I need: 1. `scores = (q_nope @ k_nope^T + q_rope @ k_rope^T) * scale` — these are dot products 2. `p = softmax(scores, dim=0)` 3. `o = p^T @ v` — weighted sum These are standard attention operations that can benefit from torch.compile or a fused kernel. But they're O(ctx × H × head_dim) which is much smaller than the weight loading, so they're not the main bottleneck on H100 at batch-1. Actually wait, on H100 at batch-1, the attention computation for ctx=16384, H=32, head_dim=128: - q_nope @ k_nope^T: (32, 128) @ (16384, 32, 128) → (32, 16384) — this is 32 independent (128,) @ (16384, 128) matmuls, total 32*16384*128 = 67M FLOPs - q_rope @ k_rope^T: (32, 64) @ (16384, 64) → (32, 16384) — 32*16384*64 = 34M FLOPs - p @ v: (32, 16384) @ (16384, 32, 128) → (32, 128) — 32*16384*128 = 67M FLOPs Total: ~168M FLOPs, which at 1000 TFLOPS (H100 bf16) is ~0.17 µs. Negligible. So the bottleneck is clearly the weight loading. The fused int4 dequant-GEMV is the key optimization. But at ctx=16384, the kv_b projection is (16384, 512) @ (512, 6144). The input/output data movement here is: - Input (c_kv): 16384 * 512 * 2 = 16.8 MB - Weight (kv_b): 512 * 6144 / 2 = 1.57 MB (int4) - Output: 16384 * 6144 * 2 = 201 MB The output is 201 MB! At 1.8 TB/s, that's ~112 µs just for writing output. With caching, we only do this once instead of at every step, saving ~1.8 ms over 16 steps. But wait — at batch-1 decode, this is a GEMM with M=ctx_len, K=512, N=6144. This is a "tall-skinny" GEMM that's compute-bound, not bandwidth-bound. Let me check the arithmetic intensity: - FLOPs: 2 * M * K * N = 2 * 16384 * 512 * 6144 ≈ 103 GFLOPS - Bytes: M*K*2 + K*N*0.5 + M*N*2 = 16.8MB + 1.57MB + 201MB ≈ 219MB - AI: 103 GFLOPS / 219 MB ≈ 470 FLOPS/byte H100 has ~312 TFLOPS (bf16) and ~2 TB/s bandwidth, so the ridge point is 312000/2000 ≈ 156 FLOPS/byte. At 470 FLOPS/byte, this is compute-bound! So the kv_b projection at ctx=16384 is compute-bound, not memory-bound. The fused int4 dequant helps but less dramatically here. The caching optimization (absorb) is what eliminates the per-step cost. For the other projections (KDA q/k/v/g/o, MLA q_proj/o_proj, MoE gate/up/down), the batch dimension is 1 (M=1), making them purely memory-bound: - FLOPs: 2 * 1 * 2304 * 4096 ≈ 18.9 MFLOPs - Bytes: 2304*2 + 4096*2304*0.531 + 4096*2 ≈ 5MB - AI: 18.9 / 5000 ≈ 3.8 FLOPS/byte — deep in bandwidth-bound regime So the fused int4 dequant-GEMV is the critical optimization for the GEMV operations (M=1), while caching is critical for the MLA kv_b GEMM (large M). OK, now let me actually write the code. Let me focus on getting a working implementation first, then optimize. Here's my plan for the solution.py: ```python """ Fused int4-dequant GEMV solution for Kimi-Linear W4A16 decode. Key optimizations: 1. Fused int4 dequant-GEMV Triton kernel - never materializes bf16 weights 2. MLA KV absorption - cache projected k_nope/v, avoid O(ctx) recomputation """ import reference as R import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F import triton import triton.language as tl Config = R.Config build_config = R.build_config init_state = R.init_state init_token = R.init_token _rmsnorm = R._rmsnorm _rope_cossin = R._rope_cossin _apply_rope = R._apply_rope GROUP_SIZE = R.GROUP_SIZE # ---- Triton fused int4 dequant-GEMV kernel ---- @triton.autotune( configs=[ triton.Config({'BLOCK_K': 64, 'BLOCK_N': 64}, num_warps=4), triton.Config({'BLOCK_K': 64, 'BLOCK_N': 128}, num_warps=4), triton.Config({'BLOCK_K': 128, 'BLOCK_N': 64}, num_warps=4), triton.Config({'BLOCK_K': 128, 'BLOCK_N': 128}, num_warps=4), triton.Config({'BLOCK_K': 256, 'BLOCK_N': 64}, num_warps=4), triton.Config({'BLOCK_K': 256, 'BLOCK_N': 128}, num_warps=8), ], key=['N', 'K', 'GROUP_SIZE'], ) @triton.jit def _fused_int4_gemv_kernel(...): ... def fused_int4_gemv(x, w_q, scales, zeros): """Fused int4 dequant-GEMV: y = x @ dequant(w_q, scales, zeros).""" K, N = w_q.shape[1] * 2, w_q.shape[2] # for QuantExperts: (E, K//2, N) -> K = (K//2)*2 # Actually, let me handle shapes properly ... ``` Wait, I need to carefully handle the shapes. For QuantLinear: - w_q: (K//2, N) - scales: (K//128, N) - zeros: (K//128, N) For QuantExperts: - w_q: (E, K//2, N) - scales: (E, K//128, N) - zeros: (E, K//128, N) For the kernel, I'll pass the flattened pointers. For a single expert, it's straightforward. For batched experts, I'll offset by the expert index. Let me write the kernel properly now. Key design decisions: 1. Grid: 1D over N dimension: `(cdiv(N, BLOCK_N),)` 2. Each program iterates over K dimension in blocks 3. Within each K block, process groups and row-pairs 4. Use fp32 accumulation for accuracy, store bf16 Actually, thinking more about it, for batch-1 GEMV, the total work is tiny (each GEMV is ~10K-40K FLOPs for the output dot products), so the kernel is purely memory-bound. The optimal tile sizes should maximize memory bandwidth utilization. For memory-bound kernels, larger tiles are better (until register pressure). Let me use: - BLOCK_K = 128 or 256 (load 64-128 bytes of w_q, 128-256 bf16 x values) - BLOCK_N = 256 or 512 (load 128-256 bytes of w_q per K row, 512-1024 bytes of scales/zeros) With BLOCK_K=256, BLOCK_N=256, each program loads: - w_q: 128 × 256 bytes = 32KB - scales/zeros: 2 × 2 × 256 × 2 bytes = 2KB (for 2 groups of 128) - x: 256 × 2 bytes = 0.5KB Total: ~35KB per K iteration At 2 TB/s, 35KB takes ~17.5 ns. With 18 groups for K=2304, total weight read is ~630KB per program, or ~315 µs. But we have many programs running in parallel... Actually, the total weight read across all programs for one GEMV at K=2304, N=4096: - w_q: 2304/2 * 4096 bytes = 4.7MB - scales/zeros: 2 * 18 * 4096 * 2 = 295KB - Total: ~5MB At 2 TB/s: ~2.5 µs for the weight reads. With ~204 SMs on H100, we can launch many blocks in parallel. The 4096/256 = 16 blocks can all run concurrently. Each block reads ~35KB per K iteration, 18 iterations = 630KB per block. 16 blocks × 630KB = 10MB total, at 2 TB/s = 5 µs. Plus compute time. These are rough estimates. The actual performance will depend on many factors. Let me just implement it and profile. Let me also think about autotuning. The kernel performance depends on N, K, and group_size. Since the dimensions vary (K from 512 to 4096, N from 576 to 6144), autotuning will help pick the right tile sizes. OK, let me write the actual code now. I'll write it step by step. For the kernel, I realize I should use a simpler, more robust approach: ```python @triton.jit def _fused_int4_gemv_kernel( w_q_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, x_ptr, y_ptr, K, N, group_size, stride_wq_k, # stride for w_q along K dimension = N (since N-contiguous) stride_sc_k, # stride for scales along group dimension = N stride_z_k, # stride for zeros along group dimension = N BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_offs = pid * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_N], dtype=tl.float32) # Process one group of GROUP_SIZE=128 rows at a time # This naturally handles the integer scaling num_groups = (K + group_size - 1) // group_size for g in range(num_groups): # Load scale and zero for this group (shared across GROUP_SIZE rows) sc_offs = g * stride_sc_k + n_offs scale = tl.load(scales_ptr + sc_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_N] zero = tl.load(zeros_ptr + sc_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_N] # Rows in this group k_start = g * group_size k_end = tl.minimum(k_start + group_size, K) # Process rows in pairs (matching packed int4 format) # Each pair of rows is stored in one byte of w_q for r in range(k_start, k_end, 2): # r = even row, r+1 = odd row (if exists) # Load x for even row x_even = tl.load(x_ptr + r).to(tl.float32) # Load x for odd row (may not exist for last row of last group) odd_exists = r + 1 < k_end x_odd = tl.load(x_ptr + r + 1, mask=odd_exists, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Load packed weight byte wq_row = r // 2 wq_offs = wq_row * stride_wq_k + n_offs wq_byte = tl.load(w_q_ptr + wq_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0) # Unpack w_even = (wq_byte & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # row r w_odd = ((wq_byte >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # row r+1 # Dequant and accumulate acc += (w_even - zero) * scale * x_even if odd_exists: acc += (w_odd - zero) * scale * x_odd # Store result tl.store(y_ptr + n_offs, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=n_mask) ``` Wait, there's a problem with this approach: the inner loop over `r` loads individual x values and w_q bytes, which doesn't use vectorized memory access. For a bandwidth-bound kernel, this is bad. Let me try a more vectorized approach where I load BLOCK_K rows of x and BLOCK_K/2 rows of w_q at once: ```python @triton.jit def _fused_int4_gemv_kernel( w_q_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, x_ptr, y_ptr, K, N, group_size, stride_wq_k, stride_sc_k, stride_z_k, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_offs = pid * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_N], dtype=tl.float32) # Process K in blocks of BLOCK_K rows for k_start in range(0, K, BLOCK_K): k_end = tl.minimum(k_start + BLOCK_K, K) num_rows = k_end - k_start # Determine which groups are covered g_start = k_start // group_size g_end = (k_end - 1) // group_size # For each group, load its scale and process its rows for g in range(g_start, g_end + 1): # Rows of this group within the K block r_start = tl.maximum(k_start, g * group_size) r_end = tl.minimum(k_end, (g + 1) * group_size) r_count = r_end - r_start # Load scale and zero for this group sc_offs = g * stride_sc_k + n_offs scale = tl.load(scales_ptr + sc_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) zero = tl.load(zeros_ptr + sc_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Process rows of this group in batches of BLOCK_K for r in range(r_start, r_end, BLOCK_K): r_sub_end = tl.minimum(r + BLOCK_K, r_end) r_sub_count = r_sub_end - r # Load x values x_offs = r + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K) x_mask = x_offs < r_sub_end x_vals = tl.load(x_ptr + x_offs, mask=x_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_K] # For each pair of x values (even, odd), load w_q and accumulate # This is the tricky part - we need to pair x[0] with nibble from w_q[r//2], # x[1] with nibble from w_q[r//2] (high nibble), etc. # Let me process in pairs within the BLOCK_K sub-block # Each iteration handles 2 rows for i in range(0, BLOCK_K, 2): if r + i >= r_sub_end: break # Even row x_even = x_vals[i] # Odd row odd_exists = r + i + 1 < r_sub_end x_odd = x_vals[i + 1] if odd_exists else 0.0 # Load w_q wq_row = (r + i) // 2 wq_offs = wq_row * stride_wq_k + n_offs wq_byte = tl.load(w_q_ptr + wq_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0) w_even = (wq_byte & 0xF).to(tl.float32) w_odd = ((wq_byte >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) acc += (w_even - zero) * scale * x_even if odd_exists: acc += (w_odd - zero) * scale * x_odd tl.store(y_ptr + n_offs, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=n_mask) ``` This still has per-row-pair w_q loads inside the loop. Let me try to truly vectorize by loading w_q tiles and doing element-wise operations. The key insight: for a block of BLOCK_K rows (which must be even), we load BLOCK_K/2 rows of w_q. Then: - w_even[i, n] = w_q_bytes[i, n] & 0xF (for even row 2*i) - w_odd[i, n] = (w_q_bytes[i, n] >> 4) & 0xF (for odd row 2*i+1) And x_even[i] = x[2*i], x_odd[i] = x[2*i+1] Then the accumulation is: ``` acc[n] += Σ_i (w_even[i,n] - zero[n]) * scale[n] * x_even[i] + (w_odd[i,n] - zero[n]) * scale[n] * x_odd[i] = scale[n] * Σ_i (w_even[i,n] * x_even[i] + w_odd[i,n] * x_odd[i] - zero[n] * (x_even[i] + x_odd[i])) = scale[n] * (Σ_i w_even[i,n] * x_even[i] + Σ_i w_odd[i,n] * x_odd[i] - zero[n] * Σ_i (x_even[i] + x_odd[i])) ``` This is more efficient if we can compute the sums in vectorized form. Let me write a version that loads full tiles: ```python @triton.jit def _fused_int4_gemv_kernel( w_q_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, x_ptr, y_ptr, K, N, group_size, stride_wq_k, stride_sc_k, stride_z_k, BLOCK_K: tl.constexpr, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, ): pid = tl.program_id(0) n_offs = pid * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_N], dtype=tl.float32) # BLOCK_K must be even for pair processing # Process groups (128 rows each) num_groups = (K + group_size - 1) // group_size for g in range(num_groups): k_start = g * group_size k_end = tl.minimum(k_start + group_size, K) # Load scale and zero sc_offs = g * stride_sc_k + n_offs scale = tl.load(scales_ptr + sc_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) zero = tl.load(zeros_ptr + sc_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Process in sub-blocks for k_sub in range(k_start, k_end, BLOCK_K): k_sub_end = tl.minimum(k_sub + BLOCK_K, k_end) num_rows = k_sub_end - k_sub # Load x (even and odd interleaved) x_even_offs = k_sub + 2 * tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2) x_odd_offs = x_even_offs + 1 num_pairs = (num_rows + 1) // 2 pair_mask = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2) < num_pairs # fix: tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K//2) x_even = tl.load(x_ptr + x_even_offs, mask=pair_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_K/2] x_odd = tl.load(x_ptr + x_odd_offs, mask=pair_mask & (x_odd_offs < k_sub_end), other=0.0).to(tl.float32) # Load w_q bytes wq_offs = (k_sub // 2 + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2))[:, None] * stride_wq_k + n_offs[None, :] wq_row_mask = (k_sub // 2 + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2)) < (K + 1) // 2 wq_mask_2d = wq_row_mask[:, None] & n_mask[None, :] wq_bytes = tl.load(w_q_ptr + wq_offs, mask=wq_mask_2d, other=0) # [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N] # Unpack w_even = (wq_bytes & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N] w_odd = ((wq_bytes >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N] # Dequantize w_even_dq = (w_even - zero[None, :]) * scale[None, :] w_odd_dq = (w_odd - zero[None, :]) * scale[None, :] # Multiply with x and sum over K dimension # w_even_dq: [BLOCK_K/2, BLOCK_N], x_even: [BLOCK_K/2] # result: [BLOCK_N] # Apply pair_mask to zero out invalid rows pair_mask_f = pair_mask.to(tl.float32) acc += tl.sum(w_even_dq * x_even[:, None] * pair_mask_f[:, None], axis=0) acc += tl.sum(w_odd_dq * x_odd[:, None] * pair_mask_f[:, None], axis=0) tl.store(y_ptr + n_offs, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=n_mask) ``` Hmm, there are still issues. The `tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2)` for `pair_mask` needs to be a proper comparison. Let me fix this. Actually, in Triton, `tl.arange(0, N)` returns a 1D tensor of values [0, 1, ..., N-1]. So `pair_mask = tl.arange(0, BLOCK_K // 2) < num_pairs` is valid. But there's a subtlety: `num_pairs` is computed as `(num_rows + 1) // 2`, and it can differ from `BLOCK_K // 2` for the last sub-block. The masking handles this. Wait, but `num_rows` could be odd. If num_rows = 1 (only one row in the sub-block), then num_pairs = 1, and we correctly process pair 0 (even row exists, odd row is masked out). Let me also think about the w_q loading mask. When K is odd (unlikely but possible), the last w_q row only has one valid nibble. But in practice, K is always even in this problem. OK I think this kernel design is workable. Let me code up the full solution now. Actually, one more optimization: for the MoE FFN, the gate and up projections have the same K and N dimensions. I could fuse them into one kernel call to amortize the x-load cost and scale/zero loads. But this makes the kernel significantly more complex. Let me start with separate calls and see if performance is good enough. Similarly, for the MLA kv_b projection when we do the initial full-cache projection, this is a GEMM (M=ctx_len, K=512, N=6144), not a GEMV. For this case, the fused int4 dequant-GEMM kernel would need a different structure (2D grid, tiling over both M and N). Let me handle this separately. So kv_b projects c_kv (shape (ctx, 512)) to (ctx, 6144), which is then split into k_nope (ctx, 32, 128) and v (ctx, 32, 128). For the caching approach: 1. It helps for benchmark.py which runs many steps. The initial cache initialization cost is paid on the first step. Actually, for check.py, the state has pre-filled c_kv (context_len tokens). With caching, we'd compute kv_b on the full context_len (initialization) plus kv_b on the new token. So it's roughly the same cost for a single step. The model is W4A16: the big projection weights are stored as 4-bit integers, the activations and accumulation are bf16. reference.py is the correctness oracle and baseline.py is an already-optimized pure-PyTorch version; your job is to go beyond baseline.py. Your answer goes in solution.py and must expose the same Model class and the same step(hidden, state) -> (hidden, state) interface as reference.py, with identical buffer/parameter names so it loads the reference weights. Make whatever mess you want in this directory otherwise.  The unit is one repeating motif of Kimi-Linear-48B-A3B: four layers in the pattern [KDA, KDA, KDA, MLA], each followed by a 64-expert MoE FFN (top-8 routed + 1 shared, SiLU, intermediate 1024), with RMSNorm and residuals. Hidden size is 2304. The KDA layers are gated-delta linear attention with 32 heads of dim 128, a short causal depthwise conv (kernel 4) on q/k/v, a per-channel log-decay gate, and a per-head write strength; they carry a fixed-size recurrent state S[32,128,128] plus the conv window across decode steps. The MLA layers are multi-head latent attention with 32 heads, a kv_lora_rank of 512, qk_nope 128, qk_rope 64, v_head 128, decoupled RoPE (theta 10000); they carry a growing compressed latent KV cache (the 512-d latent plus the 64-d rope key per past token).  The weights are quantized W4A16, AWQ/GPTQ-style: per linear, the (in, out) weight is stored as int4 packed two-per-byte along `in` (w_q, uint8, shape (in//2, out)), with per-group asymmetric dequant params group-size 128 along `in` (scales and zeros, bf16, shape (in//128, out)). Dequant is w[k,n] = (unpack(w_q)[k,n] - zeros[k//128,n]) * scales[k//128,n]. The 64 MoE experts (and the shared expert) are stored the same way, one int4 weight set per expert. reference.py has the exact pack/unpack/dequant code, the layer math, the state layout, and how step consumes one token and updates both kinds of state. Read it.  At batch 1 this decode is bandwidth-bound on the weight stream, and the int4 weights are a quarter of the bytes of bf16 - but only if you never materialize the dequantized bf16 weight. reference.py and baseline.py both unpack each weight to a full bf16 matrix and then matmul, which throws the bandwidth advantage away (int4 read plus bf16 write plus bf16 read). The job is to fuse the int4 unpack and per-group dequant directly into the GEMV so you stream the int4 weights once. You are decoding one new token against a context that is already `context_len` tokens long (the states and caches are pre-filled). Correctness is the next-token hidden vector and the updated decode state matching reference.py by cosine similarity (>= 0.98), checked from an identical fed state across several seeds and context lengths. You are scored on decode latency relative to baseline.py, geomean over context lengths 2048, 8192, and 16384.  This needs to be real. Plain bf16 matmul/linear is allowed, but you must write the fused int4 dequant-GEMV yourself - do not import a prebuilt quantized kernel (bitsandbytes, torchao, marlin, gptq/awq, exllama) or a prebuilt model/attention/MoE stack (transformers, fla, flash_mla, vllm, sglang), or reference.py / baseline.py. If you need background, look up W4A16 / AWQ / GPTQ dequant-GEMV kernels, the Kimi-Linear architecture, KDA / gated DeltaNet, multi-head latent attention, and Blackwell/Triton decode kernels yourself.  Your flywheel is implement, profile (ncu, nsys, torch.profiler - whatever helps) and time it with benchmark.py, verify correctness by running `python check.py` and reading the output, then iterate. Do not substitute your own one-off checks for check.py - it tests every layer type and the state update across seeds, your spot-check almost certainly will not. If `python check.py` has not printed PASS, you are not done. Take as long as you need to actually push the latency down. reasoning
There's a shape mismatch. `zeros` has shape (ng, N) = (18, 4096). After `.float().view(ng, 1, N)` it would be (18, 1, 4096). The error says "shape '[18, 1, 4096]' is invalid for input of size 1327104" which is 18 * 4096 = 73728 — but wait, 18*4096 = 73728, not 1327104. Hmm, 1327104 = 18 * 4096 * 18? No, 1327104 / 18 = 73728, / 4096 = 18. So the tensor has shape (18, 18, 4096)? That can't be right. Let me check: `scales` is (18, 4096). `.float()` keeps the shape. `.view(ng, 1, N)` should work since ng=18, N=4096. Wait, maybe `scales` has a different shape because it was constructed incorrectly. This is surprising. Let me think about why. The baseline approach is: 1. Unpack int4 to uint8 (K, N) — reads K*N/2 bytes, writes K*N bytes 2. Expand scales/zeros (K/128, N) → (K, N) — reads small, writes K*N*2 bytes each (bf16) 3. Dequant: (uint8_to_bf16 - zero_bf16) * scale_bf16 → K*N bf16 reads + writes 4. GEMV: x @ w → reads K*N*2 + K*2 bytes, writes N*2 bytes The baseline uses cuBLAS for step 4, which is highly optimized. My fused kernel: - Loops over groups (18) and sub-blocks within each group - Per sub-block: loads x, w_q bytes, scales, zeros - Does the computation with Triton-generated code The issue is likely that my kernel has poor memory access patterns or poor occupancy. Let me think about what might be slow: 1. The kernel is processing a single GEMV (M=1, K=2304, N=4096). The grid has cdiv(4096, 128) = 32 blocks, each with 4 warps. That's only 128 warps for the whole thing — very low occupancy on H100's 132 SMs. 2. Each block does 18 group iterations, and within each group, sub-blocks of KPAIR=32 pairs. For K=2304, each group has 128 rows = 64 pairs = 2 sub-blocks of 32 pairs. So total iterations: 18 groups × (128 rows/group) / (64 rows/sub-block) = 36 inner iterations. 3. Each inner iteration does: load x (64 floats), load w_q (32*128 = 4096 bytes), load scales/zeros (2*128 floats = 256 bytes each), compute, accumulate. 4. The memory access pattern for w_q: loads a [32, 128] tile (4096 bytes). At 2 TB/s, that's 2 µs per load. 36 iterations × 2 µs = 72 µs just for weight reads. Plus compute and overhead. That's close to the 87.8 µs we see. 5. The baseline: PyTorch unpacks (vectorized memory ops), does dequant (fused pointwise ops), then calls cuBLAS GEMV which has highly optimized memory access. Total: 13.7 µs. So the problem is that the Triton kernel is doing the right thing but with too many small loads and low parallelism. The key issue: for M=1 (GEMV), the compute is so tiny that the launch overhead and memory patterns dominate. Accumulate Each of these is a separate memory operation, and Triton doesn't pipeline them well across loop iterations. 18 iterations of this overhead adds up. Let me try a radically different approach: instead of a group loop, flatten the computation into a single reduction over K with dynamic scale/zero loading. Actually, here's an idea: use torch.compile on a simple Python loop over K elements. torch.compile might generate an efficient CUDA kernel: ```python @torch.compile def fused_gemv(x, w_q, scales, zeros, K, N, group_size): y = torch.zeros(N, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=x.device) for k in range(K): wq_byte = w_q[k//2, :] if k % 2 == 0: w_val = (wq_byte & 0xF).to(torch.bfloat16) else: w_val = ((wq_byte >> 4) & 0xF).to(torch.bfloat16) g = k // group_size w_deq = (w_val.float() - zeros[g, :].float()) * scales[g, :].float() y += x[k].float() * w_deq return y.to(torch.bfloat16) ``` But this is a Python loop with 2304 iterations — torch.compile won't efficiently compile per-element loops. Actually, torch.compile can handle element-wise operations well with `torch.vmap` or `torch.einsum`. Let me think... Actually, for a GEMV, the operation is: y[n] = Σ_k w_deq[k,n] * x[k]. This can be written as a sum over k of (x[k] * scale[k//128, :] * (unpack(w_q[k//2, :])[k%2, :] - zeros[k//128, :])). Or more efficiently with block operations: ```python # For each group (128 K rows): for g in range(num_groups): k_slice = slice(g*128, (g+1)*128) x_g = x[k_slice] # (128,) s_g = scales[g:g+1] # (1, N) z_g = zeros[g:g+1] # (1, N) # Unpack w_q for this group: w_q[g*64:(g+1)*64, :] has 64 rows, each = 2 K rows wq_bytes = w_q[g*64:(g+1)*64, :] # (64, N) w_even = (wq_bytes & 0xF).to(torch.bfloat16) # (64, N) w_odd = ((wq_bytes >> 4) & 0xF).to(torch.bfloat16) # (64, N) x_even = x_g[0::2] # (64,) x_odd = x_g[1::2] # (64,) # y += (w_even - z_g) * s_g * x_even[:, None] + (w_odd - z_g) * s_g * x_odd[:, None] # This is a GEMV-like operation y += ((w_even.float() - z_g.float()) * s_g.float()).T @ x_even.float() y += ((w_odd.float() - z_g.float()) * s_g.float()).T @ x_odd.float() ``` But this materializes (64, N) tensors for each group, which is only 64/128 = half the full matrix. 18 groups × 64 × 4096 × 2 bytes = 9.4 MB — still better than 18.9 MB for the full matrix. Actually, the key insight is that this IS better than the full dequant because we never materialize the full (K, N) matrix at once. We process one group at a time and accumulate. But the question is: can PyTorch/cuBLAS GEMV handle (64, 4096) @ (64,) efficiently? Yes — it's just 18 GEMV calls instead of one big dequant + one GEMV. Total data per group: read 64×4096/2 = 128 KB packed, write 64×4096×2 = 512 KB for w_even + 512 KB for w_odd. Then 2 GEMV calls each reading 512 KB. Hmm, this is 18 groups × (128 KB read + 1024 KB write + 1024 KB read) = 18 × 2176 KB = 39 MB total. The cuBLAS GEMV is very fast though. Let me try this "group-wise dequant + matmul" approach and see how it performs. Each group processes 128 K rows out of 2304, so 18 groups. Each group does partial dequant on 64 K-pairs and two GEMVs. Actually, let me try yet another approach: use the fact that cuBLAS is very fast for the GEMV part. Instead of trying to beat cuBLAS with my Triton GEMV, I can: 1. Process one group at a time 2. For each group, unpack 128 K rows to bf16 (partial dequant — only 1/18 of the full matrix) 3. Do the GEMV contribution: y += x_g @ w_g_deq 4. Accumulate into the output This way, I only ever materialize 128/2304 ≈ 5.6% of the full weight matrix at once, and I leverage cuBLAS for the GEMV part. For K=2304, N=4096: - Per group: unpack 64 bytes → 128 uint8 rows → 128 bf16 rows - Read: 64 × 4096 = 256 KB packed - Write: 128 × 4096 × 2 = 1 MB bf16 - Expand scales/zeros: read small, write 1 MB each (or broadcast in GEMV) - Two GEMVs (even and odd): 2 × (1 MB read + output write) - Total per group: ~256 KB + 1 MB + 2×1 MB + ~4 MB for GEMVs = ~7.3 MB - 18 groups: 131 MB That's actually similar to the full dequant! The full dequant materializes 18.9 MB once, then the GEMV reads 18.9 MB. Total: ~19 MB + ~19 MB = 38 MB. Per group approach: 7.3 × 18 = 131 MB. Much worse! So the group-wise approach with cuBLAS is WORSE because it re-reads scales/zeros and has more overhead. The correct approach is to read the int4 weights once and never write intermediate bf16 data. That's what the fused Triton kernel does. But my kernel is too slow. Let me think about what's limiting the Triton kernel performance. Maybe I should use `tl.dot` with a weight matrix represented in a creative way. Or use multiple program axes. Actually, here's another idea: use a 2D grid where one axis handles groups and the other handles output elements. Each (group, N-tile) block: 1. Loads w_q for this group and N-tile (64 × BLOCK_N bytes) 2. Loads x for this group (128 floats) 3. Loads scale/zero for this group and N-tile (BLOCK_N floats each) 4. Computes the partial dot product 5. Atomically adds to the output Then no outer loop over groups — each block handles exactly one group. With 18 groups and 64 N-blocks (for BLOCK_N=64), we have 1152 blocks. This improves occupancy massively. Let me write this kernel.

+"""Test torch.compile fusion of dequant+matmul."""
+
+import torch
+import time
+
+def _unpack_int4_ref(w_q_packed, K):
+    out = torch.empty((K, w_q_packed.shape[1]), dtype=torch.uint8, device=w_q_packed.device)
+    out[0::2] = w_q_packed & 0xF
+    out[1::2] = (w_q_packed >> 4) & 0xF
+    return out
+
+def dequant_ref(w_q, scales, zeros, K, group=128):
+    wu = _unpack_int4_ref(w_q, K).to(torch.bfloat16)

reasoning
Let me completely rewrite solution.py with a clean, working kernel design. The key insight from testing: 1. V2 kernel with BLOCK_N=64, GROUP_SIZE=128 constexpr: 32.6 µs (fastest) 2. torch.compile on naive dequant+matmul: 96.5 µs 3. Naive dequant+matmul: 409 µs My plan: - Use the V2 kernel approach (group-wise processing, one group per inner iteration, BLOCK_N configurable) - Add MLA KV absorption caching - Keep the rest of the structure clean Let me also add torch.compile to the small non-quantized operations (norms, convs, etc.) for extra speed. Actually, let me focus on correctness first. Let me make sure the kernel works correctly, then optimize. The V2 kernel that worked: ```python @triton.jit def _gemv_kernel( w_q_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, x_ptr, y_ptr, K, stride_wq_k, stride_sc_k, BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, GROUP_SIZE: tl.constexpr, N: tl.constexpr, ): HALF_GROUP: tl.constexpr = GROUP_SIZE // 2 pid = tl.program_id(0) n_offs = pid * BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) n_mask = n_offs < N acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_N], dtype=tl.float32) num_groups = K // GROUP_SIZE for g in range(num_groups): k_start = g * GROUP_SIZE sc_offs = g * stride_sc_k + n_offs scale = tl.load(scales_ptr + sc_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) zero = tl.load(zeros_ptr + sc_offs, mask=n_mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) x_even_offs = k_start + 2 * tl.arange(0, HALF_GROUP) x_odd_offs = x_even_offs + 1 x_even = tl.load(x_ptr + x_even_offs, mask=x_even_offs < K, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) x_odd = tl.load(x_ptr + x_odd_offs, mask=x_odd_offs < K, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) wq_k_start = k_start // 2 wq_k_offs = wq_k_start + tl.arange(0, HALF_GROUP) wq_2d_offs = wq_k_offs[:, None] * stride_wq_k + n_offs[None, :] wq_2d_mask = (wq_k_offs < (K // 2))[:, None] & n_mask[None, :] wq_bytes = tl.load(w_q_ptr + wq_2d_offs, mask=wq_2d_mask, other=0) w_even = (wq_bytes & 0xF).to(tl.float32) w_odd = ((wq_bytes >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) acc += tl.sum((w_even - zero[None, :]) * scale[None, :] * x_even[:, None], axis=0) acc += tl.sum((w_odd - zero[None, :]) * scale[None, :] * x_odd[:, None], axis=0) tl.store(y_ptr + n_offs, acc.to(tl.bfloat16), mask=n_mask) ``` The issue with this kernel is that N and BLOCK_N must be powers of 2 for `tl.arange`. Let me check: 4096 = 2^12 ✓, 6144 = not power of 2, 2304 = not power of 2, 576 = not power of 2, 1024 = 2^10 ✓. So N=6144, 2304, 576 will fail with `tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)` where BLOCK_N is a power of 2 but N is not. Wait, no — `tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)` requires BLOCK_N to be a power of 2. N is used only for the mask (`n_offs < N`), which is a runtime check. The arange only depends on BLOCK_N which IS a power of 2. So the issue is only that BLOCK_N must be a power of 2. 64 and 128 are powers of 2, so those work. The N dimension can be anything; the mask handles the boundary. OK so the kernel should work. Let me now rewrite solution.py. For the V2 kernel, I need to handle: 1. K not being a multiple of GROUP_SIZE (shouldn't happen in this problem but for robustness) 2. GROUP_SIZE and N as constexpr (annoying for the Python interface) 3. The kernel needs to be wrapped to handle non-power-of-2 N for BLOCK_N selection For the interface, I'll create a Python wrapper that: 1. Checks N/K/group constraints 2. Picks BLOCK_N (power of 2 ≤ 256, ≤ N) 3. Launches the kernel For the autotuning, I can use a manual grid search instead of `triton.autotune` since we need N to be constexpr (which causes issues with autotune). Actually, wait. Looking at the original kernel in solution.py, the autotune key includes N. When N is in the autotune key, it becomes a compile-time constant for each variant. So autotune with `key=["N", "K", "group_size"]` SHOULD make N available as constexpr. But the issue is: `triton.autotune` in Triton 3.6 might not propagate key parameters as constexpr to the JIT function body. Let me test this. Actually, looking at Triton source code, when you use `key=["N"]`, the autotuner generates separate JITFunction instances for each unique N value. In each instance, N is baked in as a constant (not a dynamic argument). But the kernel function signature still receives N as a regular argument — the baking happens at the autotune dispatch level. But the kernel body uses `tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)` where BLOCK_N is a `tl.constexpr` parameter. N is used in `n_offs < N` which is a runtime comparison. So N doesn't need to be constexpr — only BLOCK_N does. OK, so the issue is only with `tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N)` — as long as BLOCK_N is a constexpr parameter (which it is), it works regardless of N. Let me consolidate and write the final solution.py. I'll use a manually-tuned approach with the V2 kernel and pre-select good BLOCK_N values based on N.
