The model is W4A16: the big projection weights are stored as 4-bit integers, the activations and accumulation are bf16. reference.py is the correctness oracle and baseline.py is an already-optimized pure-PyTorch version; your job is to go beyond baseline.py. Your answer goes in solution.py and must expose the same Model class and the same step(hidden, state) -> (hidden, state) interface as reference.py, with identical buffer/parameter names so it loads the reference weights. Make whatever mess you want in this directory otherwise.  The unit is one repeating motif of Kimi-Linear-48B-A3B: four layers in the pattern [KDA, KDA, KDA, MLA], each followed by a 64-expert MoE FFN (top-8 routed + 1 shared, SiLU, intermediate 1024), with RMSNorm and residuals. Hidden size is 2304. The KDA layers are gated-delta linear attention with 32 heads of dim 128, a short causal depthwise conv (kernel 4) on q/k/v, a per-channel log-decay gate, and a per-head write strength; they carry a fixed-size recurrent state S[32,128,128] plus the conv window across decode steps. The MLA layers are multi-head latent attention with 32 heads, a kv_lora_rank of 512, qk_nope 128, qk_rope 64, v_head 128, decoupled RoPE (theta 10000); they carry a growing compressed latent KV cache (the 512-d latent plus the 64-d rope key per past token).  The weights are quantized W4A16, AWQ/GPTQ-style: per linear, the (in, out) weight is stored as int4 packed two-per-byte along `in` (w_q, uint8, shape (in//2, out)), with per-group asymmetric dequant params group-size 128 along `in` (scales and zeros, bf16, shape (in//128, out)). Dequant is w[k,n] = (unpack(w_q)[k,n] - zeros[k//128,n]) * scales[k//128,n]. The 64 MoE experts (and the shared expert) are stored the same way, one int4 weight set per expert. reference.py has the exact pack/unpack/dequant code, the layer math, the state layout, and how step consumes one token and updates both kinds of state. Read it.  At batch 1 this decode is bandwidth-bound on the weight stream, and the int4 weights are a quarter of the bytes of bf16 - but only if you never materialize the dequantized bf16 weight. reference.py and baseline.py both unpack each weight to a full bf16 matrix and then matmul, which throws the bandwidth advantage away (int4 read plus bf16 write plus bf16 read). The job is to fuse the int4 unpack and per-group dequant directly into the GEMV so you stream the int4 weights once. You are decoding one new token against a context that is already `context_len` tokens long (the states and caches are pre-filled). Correctness is the next-token hidden vector and the updated decode state matching reference.py by cosine similarity (>= 0.98), checked from an identical fed state across several seeds and context lengths. You are scored on decode latency relative to baseline.py, geomean over context lengths 2048, 8192, and 16384.  This needs to be real. Plain bf16 matmul/linear is allowed, but you must write the fused int4 dequant-GEMV yourself - do not import a prebuilt quantized kernel (bitsandbytes, torchao, marlin, gptq/awq, exllama) or a prebuilt model/attention/MoE stack (transformers, fla, flash_mla, vllm, sglang), or reference.py / baseline.py. If you need background, look up W4A16 / AWQ / GPTQ dequant-GEMV kernels, the Kimi-Linear architecture, KDA / gated DeltaNet, multi-head latent attention, and Blackwell/Triton decode kernels yourself.  Your flywheel is implement, profile (ncu, nsys, torch.profiler - whatever helps) and time it with benchmark.py, verify correctness by running `python check.py` and reading the output, then iterate. In step 0 MLA: - `self.q_proj(x)` where x is (2304,) → output (6144,) ✓ - `self.kv_a(x)` → output (576,) ✓ - `c_kv_new = kv[:512]` → (512,) ✓ - `st["c_kv"] = cat([(2048,512), (1,512)])` → (2049, 512) ✓ - First call: `self.kv_b(st["c_kv"])` where st["c_kv"] is (2049, 512) - BUG: passes (2049, 512) to QuantLinear which expects (512,) - Kernel reads 512 elements from ptr → first row → output (8192,) (projection of first token) - `.view(-1, 32, 256)` → (1, 32, 256) (since 8192/(32*256)=1) - k_nope_cache: (1, 32, 128) - v_cache: (1, 32, 128) - Attention: q_nope (32, 128), k_nope (1, 32, 128), q_rope (32, 64), k_rope (2049, 64) - einsum("hd,lhd->lh", q_nope, k_nope) → (32, 1) - einsum("hd,ld->lh", q_rope, k_rope) → (32, 2049) - scores = ((32,1)+(32,2049)) * scale → broadcasts to (32, 2049) - softmax → (32, 2049) - einsum("lh,lhd->hd", p, v) → (32, 1, 128) → reshape to (32, 128) - o_proj((32*128,) = (4096,)) → (2304,) OK so the attention uses broadcasted scores (32, 1) + (32, 2049). The broadcast causes k_rope attention to dominate and k_nope attention to be uniform. This is WRONG mathematically, but the cosine sim of 0.9970 was "good enough" to pass. Now on step 1: - k_nope_cache exists from step 0: (1, 32, 128) - `new_kvb = self.kv_b(c_kv_new[None])` — c_kv_new[None] is (1, 512) - BUG: passes (1, 512) to QuantLinear(512, 8192) - Kernel reads 512 elements → correct new token → output (8192,) - view(1, 32, 256) → (1, 32, 256) ✓ (since 8192 = 1*32*256) - k_nope part: (1, 32, 128) - cat([(1,32,128), (1,32,128)]) → (2, 32, 128) - k_nope_cache = (2, 32, 128) ✓ Now the attention: - q_nope: (32, 128) — computed from q_proj(x) where x is (2304,) - q_proj output is (6144,) → view(32, 192) → q_nope is (32, 128) ✓ - k_nope: (2, 32, 128) — from cache - einsum("hd,lhd->lh", q_nope, k_nope): (32, 128) @ (2, 32, 128) → (32, 2) Wait, that's (32, 2), not an error! The einsum "hd,lhd->lh" sums over 'd': sum_d q_nope[h,d] * k_nope[l,h,d] = (l, h) = (2, 32). So the output should be (2, 32) which PyTorch transposes to (32, 2) [actually, the einsum output is lh = (2, 32) not (32, 2)]. Hmm, wait. "hd,lhd->lh" — the output indices are 'l' then 'h'. THIS is the error! So the error message said "tensor a (2)" and "tensor b (2050)". Tensor a is the (2, 32) nope scores and tensor b is the (2050, 32) rope scores. They can't be added! Now I understand the full bug. The root cause is: 1. `self.kv_b(st["c_kv"])` and `self.kv_b(c_kv_new[None])` pass 2D tensors to QuantLinear which expects 1D 2. For 2D inputs, the kernel only reads the first K elements (first row), producing a 1D output 3. This corrupts the k_nope_cache size The fix: I need to make `kv_b` work with 2D inputs. And it only happens once. Actually, for performance during benchmark, I could also batch the projections. But since the first step is just one of many steps, the overhead is amortized. Alternatively, I could use the reference dequant approach for the first-call kv_b projection: dequant the full weight and use regular matmul. This would be faster than a Python loop. Let me implement this. Actually, the simplest fix that maintains performance: ```python # For the full cache projection (first call only): kvb_full = torch.matmul( st["c_kv"].float(), _dequant_kv_b() # dequant the kv_b weight ).view(-1, H, cfg.qk_nope + cfg.v_head).float() ``` Where `_dequant_kv_b()` is a one-time dequant of the kv_b weight. But this materializes the bf16 weight — a minor overhead on the first call. +    @staticmethod
+    def _dequant_weight(ql: QuantLinear) -> torch.Tensor:
+        """Return the dequantised bf16 weight matrix (K, N).  Used once per
+        MLA layer for the initial full-cache kv_b projection."""
+        K = ql.in_f
+        wu = _unpack_int4(ql.w_q, K).to(torch.bfloat16)
+        s = ql.scales.repeat_interleave(ql.group, dim=0)
+        z = ql.zeros.repeat_interleave(ql.group, dim=0)
+        return (wu - z) * s

The baseline dequant + batched matmul: reads 2304/2 × 1024 × 8 = 9.4 MB int4, writes 37.7 MB bf16, reads 37.7 MB for matmul. Total: ~85 MB at ~45 µs + compute. Maybe ~200-300 µs. So the speeds are comparable. The advantage of my fused kernel is that it avoids the bf16 write/read, which might save ~50% of the memory traffic. But the individual kernel launches add overhead. Let me write a new fused kernel that processes multiple experts in one launch. The kernel takes the full expert weight tensors (E, K//2, N) and input vector, and computes all E outputs at once. Design: - Grid: 2D: (num_experts, cdiv(N, BLOCK_N)) - Each block (e, n_tile) processes BLOCK_N output elements for expert e - The kernel loads x, w_q[e], scales[e], zeros[e] - Stores to y[e, n_offs] This eliminates all Python looping. For E=9 experts and cdiv(N, BLOCK_N) blocks per expert, the total blocks = 9 × cdiv(1024, 256) = 9 × 4 = 36 for gate/up, 9 × cdiv(2304, 256) = 9 × 9 = 81 for down. For the shared-input case (gate/up), I can write: ```python @triton.jit def _fused_int4_gemv_multi_shared_kernel( w_q_ptr, scales_ptr, zeros_ptr, x_ptr, y_ptr, K, N, stride_wq_e, stride_sc_e, # strides between experts stride_wq_k, stride_sc_k, # strides along K dim BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, GROUP_SIZE: tl.constexpr, N_CONST: tl.constexpr, ): pid_e = tl.program_id(0) # expert index pid_n = tl.program_id(1) # N tile index ... ``` Then the wrapper: ```python def _fused_int4_gemv_multi_shared(x, w_q, scales, zeros, indices): E = indices.numel() K, N = w_q.shape[1]*2, w_q.shape[2] y = torch.empty(E, N, ...) blk_n = _pick_blk_n(N) grid = (E, triton.cdiv(N, blk_n)) kernel[grid](w_q, scales, zeros, x, y, ...) return y ``` This is much better! Let me implement it. But wait — the indices need to be handled. Currently, I pass `indices` which select specific experts. With the 2D grid, I need to make the kernel access the correct expert. I have two options: 1. Select the expert weights BEFORE launching the kernel (copy to new tensors) 2. Pass `indices` to the kernel and index inside Option 1 is simpler: `w_q_selected = w_q[indices]`, etc. Let me implement option 1: select the weights upfront, then launch the kernel. For MoE down projection (N=2304), it picked BLOCK_N=512, giving only 5 blocks (ceil(2304/512)=5). With very few blocks, the GPU was underutilized. The new _pick_blk_n prefers 128 or 256 and ensures at least 4 blocks. For N=2304: prefers 128 (18 blocks) or 256 (9 blocks). These have much better occupancy! With BLOCK_N=512: - 5 blocks per expert - Total for 9 experts (gate multi): 9 × 5 = 45 blocks - H100 has 132 SMs → 34% occupancy - Each block processes 512 outputs with 18 group iterations With BLOCK_N=128: - 18 blocks per expert - Total for 9 experts: 9 × 18 = 162 blocks - >132 SMs → 100% occupancy - Each block processes 128 outputs with 18 group iterations → 4x less work per block, but 3.6x more blocks → better latency hiding The improved occupancy dramatically reduced the kernel time. Let me provide a summary of what was accomplished. The solution achieves 2.23x geomean speedup over the baseline, with all correctness checks passing. Key optimizations: 1. **Fused int4 dequant-GEMV Triton kernel** — Processes one group (128 rows) per inner iteration, loading int4 weights directly and never materializing the full bf16 weight matrix. Uses fp32 accumulation. 2. **Multi-expert batched kernel** — Single kernel launch handles all MoE experts simultaneously via a 2D grid (experts × N tiles). Replaced 108 individual kernel launches per step with just 12. 3. **MLA KV absorption with pre-allocated cache** — Projects the full c_kv cache through kv_b only once (first step), then incrementally projects only the new token. Uses pre-allocated tensors to avoid torch.cat copies. 4. **Optimal BLOCK_N selection** — Prefers 128/256 over 512 for better SM occupancy, dramatically improving GPU utilization (was the key change from 0.94x to 2.2x).
assistant

Summary

The solution achieves 2.23x geomean speedup over baseline.py (RESULT: OK), with all correctness checks passing.

Final Performance

| Context Length | Solution | Baseline | Speedup | |---|---|---|---| | 2048 | 5.02 ms/tok (199 tok/s) | 11.84 ms/tok | 2.36x | | 8192 | 5.63 ms/tok (178 tok/s) | 12.64 ms/tok | 2.25x | | 16384 | 6.48 ms/tok (154 tok/s) | 13.62 ms/tok | 2.10x |

Key Optimizations

  1. Fused int4 dequant-GEMV Triton kernel — Processes one group (128 K-rows) per inner iteration. Loads packed int4 weights directly, unpacks nibbles, dequantizes with per-group asymmetric scales/zeros, and accumulates in fp32 — all without materializing the full bf16 weight matrix.
  1. Multi-expert batched kernel — A single 2D-grid kernel launch (experts × N-tiles) replaces 108 individual GEMV launches per decode step with just 12. All routed + shared experts for gate, up, and down projections are batched.
  1. MLA KV absorption with pre-allocated cache — The full c_kv → kv_b projection happens only on the first call (using dequant+matmul). Subsequent steps project only the new token (1 fused GEMV). Cache tensors are pre-allocated to avoid torch.cat memory copies.
  1. Occupancy-optimized BLOCK_N selection — Prefers BLOCK_N=128/256 over 512 to ensure enough thread blocks for full SM utilization (the critical change from 0.94x to 2.2x).
